Thus F = A + μ v, with μ = (2 Xp (r1+1) + 2 r1^2 - 2 x0) / |v|^2.

We'll set M = (0,0), N = (0,d). Without loss of generality, we can scale so that d = 1. So M = (0,0), N = (0,1). Then C = (0, -r1), D = (0, 1 + r2). Then A = (x0, y0) with y0 = (1 + r1^2 - r2^2)/2. And x0^2 = r1^2 - y0^2.

Now compute Yp = (1 + r2 - r1)/2. And Xp is computed as:

Xp = [ x0^2 + (Yp - y0)^2 - (Yp + r1)^2 ] / (2 x0). Simplify numerator:

We have (Yp - y0)^2 - (Yp + r1)^2 = (Yp^2 - 2 Yp y0 + y0^2) - (Yp^2 + 2 Yp r1 + r1^2) = -2 Yp y0 + y0^2 - 2 Yp r1 - r1^2.

Thus numerator N = x0^2 - 2 Yp (y0 + r1) + y0^2 - r1^2.

But y0^2 - r1^2 = -x0^2 (since x0^2 + y0^2 = r1^2 => y0^2 - r1^2 = -x0^2). So

N = x0^2 - 2 Yp (y0 + r1) - x0^2 = -2 Yp (y0 + r1).

Thus Xp = [ -2 Yp (y0 + r1) ] / (2 x0) = - Yp (y0 + r1) / x0.

So Xp = - Yp (y0 + r1) / x0.

Now note that Yp > 0. x0 could be positive or negative; but A is one intersection, we can assume A is to the right of MN (positive x), so x0 > 0. That's okay. Then Xp is negative because of the minus sign. So P is to the left of the y-axis. That's okay. In the numeric scaled example (d=1), we had A = (0, 3/4) after scaling? That gave x0=0, which is degenerate? Actually earlier with d=4, after scaling to d=1, we got A = (0, 3/4). That's x0=0, which is not allowed because A would be on MN. So that configuration is not generic? But in the numeric example, A was (0,3) with d=4. That's x0=0. That's a special case where A lies on MN (since MN is horizontal). In that example, AB is vertical. That's fine. In our vertical MN coordinate, that would correspond to A having x0 = 0? But we argued that A cannot be on MN. In that numeric example, MN is horizontal. So A being on MN? Actually in numeric example, M=(0,0), N=(4,0). A was (0,3). That's not on MN (x-axis) because MN is the x-axis; A's y-coordinate is 3, not 0, so A is not on MN. So it's fine. In our vertical MN coordinate, A would have x-coordinate non-zero. So generic case is x0 ≠ 0. So we can assume x0 ≠ 0. That's consistent.

Now we have P = (Xp, Yp) with Xp negative. That's fine.

Now H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since MN is vertical, the altitude from P is horizontal, so H lies on y = Yp. Also altitude from M (or N) determines h_x. Let's compute H.

M = (0,0), N = (0,1), P = (Xp, Yp). Compute H = (h_x, Yp). To find h_x, use that H lies on altitude from M, which is line through M perpendicular to PN. PN = (Xp - 0, Yp - 1) = (Xp, Yp - 1). A direction vector perpendicular to PN is (Yp - 1, -Xp). So param: M + t*(Yp - 1, -Xp). Set y-coordinate = Yp => t*(Yp - 1) = Yp => t = Yp / (Yp - 1). Then h_x = t * ( -Xp )? Actually param: (0,0) + t*(Yp - 1, -Xp) = (t*(Yp - 1), -t Xp). So y-coordinate is t*(Yp - 1). Setting equal to Yp gives t = Yp / (Yp - 1). Then h_x = -t Xp = - (Yp / (Yp - 1)) * Xp.

Now we also could use N similarly. But this is okay. Note that Yp - 1 could be negative or positive? Yp = (1 + r2 - r1)/2. Since r2 - r1 < 1, Yp < 1. Also Yp > 0. So Yp - 1 is negative. That's fine.

Now we have H = (h_x, Yp). So H lies on the horizontal line y = Yp, which is the same as the line containing P (since P also has y = Yp). So P and H have same y-coordinate. So PH is horizontal. Good.

Now the line L through H parallel to AP: AP direction is v = P - A = (Xp - x0, Yp - y0). So L has direction v. Since H is on y = Yp, L is a line with that slope.

Now we need to prove L is tangent to circle (BEF). B = (-x0, y0) (reflection of A across y-axis). So B is known.

Now we have expressions for A, B, P, H, Yp, Xp. We also need E and F. Since A is on Ω and Γ, we can compute E and F as second intersections of line AP with those circles. That involves solving parametric equations. That seems similar difficulty. However perhaps the symmetry with vertical MN makes the computation of E and F simpler? Let's try to compute λ and μ.

We have A = (x0, y0). P = (Xp, Yp). v = P - A.

We have t_E = -2 (A·v) / |v|^2. A·v = A·P - r1^2. We already have A·P = -r1 Xp (since that's independent of orientation). But careful: In this coordinate, M = (0,0), so A·P = x0 Xp + y0 Yp. We can compute A·P using the derived relation? Previously we derived A·P = -r1 Xp with the earlier orientation (MN horizontal). That derivation used that P is circumcenter of ACD and that C = (-r1,0) and D = (d+r2,0) and that A·P = -r1 Xp (where Xp is the x-coordinate of P). That derivation was based on Yp = - Xp (x0 + r1) / y0. In the current coordinate, we have a different orientation; but we can derive a similar relation. Let's do it.

We have M = (0,0), N = (0,d). Then C = (0, -r1), D = (0, d + r2). P = (Xp, Yp). Since P is circumcenter of ACD, we have (Xp - x0)^2 + (Yp - y0)^2 = (Xp - 0)^2 + (Yp + r1)^2 (distance to C). That gives:

(Xp - x0)^2 + (Yp - y0)^2 = Xp^2 + (Yp + r1)^2. Expand:

Xp^2 - 2 Xp x0 + x0^2 + Yp^2 - 2 Yp y0 + y0^2 = Xp^2 + Yp^2 + 2 Yp r1 + r1^2.

Cancel Xp^2, Yp^2:

-2 Xp x0 + x0^2 - 2 Yp y0 + y0^2 = 2 Yp r1 + r1^2.

But we know x0^2 + y0^2 = r1^2. So substitute:

-2 Xp x0 - 2 Yp y0 + r1^2 = 2 Yp r1 + r1^2.

Cancel r1^2:

-2 Xp x0 - 2 Yp y0 = 2 Yp r1.

Divide by 2:

- Xp x0 - Yp y0 = Yp r1.

Thus Xp x0 + Yp y0 = - Yp r1.

So A·P = - Yp r1.

That's a similar expression but with Yp instead of Xp. So indeed A·P = - r1 Yp.

Now compute A·v = A·P - |A|^2 = - r1 Yp - r1^2 = - r1 (Yp + r1). So t_E = -2 * ( - r1 (Yp + r1) ) / |v|^2 = 2 r1 (Yp + r1) / |v|^2. Good.

Now for Γ: N = (0, d) = (0,1) after scaling. A·(P - A) is not needed. Compute (A - N)·v. A - N = (x0, y0 - 1). Then (A - N)·v = (A - N)·(P - A) = (A - N)·P - (A - N)·A.

Compute (A - N)·P = A·P - N·P = -r1 Yp - (0 * Xp + 1 * Yp) = -r1 Yp - Yp = -Yp (r1 + 1). N·P = (0,1)·(Xp, Yp) = Yp.

Thus (A - N)·P = -r1 Yp - Yp = -Yp (r1 + 1). Then (A - N)·A = |A|^2 - N·A = r1^2 - (0 * x0 + 1 * y0) = r1^2 - y0. So (A - N)·v = -Yp (r1 + 1) - (r1^2 - y0) = -Yp (r1 + 1) - r1^2 + y0.

Thus t_F = -2 * ( (A - N)·v ) / |v|^2 = (2 Yp (r1 + 1) + 2 r1^2 - 2 y0) / |v|^2.

Now note that y0 is something. So that's the expression.

|P|^2 = Yp^2 * ( 2 r1 (r1 + y0) ) / x0^2.

Now also |v|^2 = |P|^2 + r1^2 + 2 r1 Yp. So

Now note that we also have expression for Xp x0 + Yp y0 = - r1 Yp. That's already used.

Let's attempt a more systematic analytic approach with d=1 and MN horizontal. We'll compute everything in terms of r1,r2. We'll need to simplify expressions like (x0 + r1) and y0. Let's compute y0^2 explicitly.

We have:

y0^2 = r1^2 - x0^2 = r1^2 - ((1 + r1^2 - r2^2)^2 / 4). 

Simplify:

4 r1^2 - (1 + r1^2 - r2^2)^2 = 4 r1^2 - ( (r1^2 - r2^2) + 1 )^2 = 4 r1^2 - ( (r1^2 - r2^2)^2 + 2 (r1^2 - r2^2) + 1 ).

So

y0^2 = [4 r1^2 - (r1^2 - r2^2)^2 - 2 (r1^2 - r2^2) - 1] / 4.

Now (r1^2 - r2^2)^2 = (r2^2 - r1^2)^2. So

y0^2 = [4 r1^2 - (r2^2 - r1^2)^2 - 2(r1^2 - r2^2) - 1] / 4.

But (r1^2 - r2^2) = -(r2^2 - r1^2). So -2(r1^2 - r2^2) = 2(r2^2 - r1^2). Then

y0^2 = [4 r1^2 - (r2^2 - r1^2)^2 + 2(r2^2 - r1^2) - 1] / 4.

That's symmetric? Not obviously.

We can also use the symmetric expression:

y0^2 = ( (r1 + r2)^2 - 1 ) * ( 1 - (r2 - r1)^2 ) / 4. That's nice. Since d=1, that's

y0^2 = \frac{ ((r1 + r2)^2 - 1) (1 - (r2 - r1)^2) }{4}.

That's a product. This might be easier to handle.

Similarly, x0 = (1 + r1^2 - r2^2)/2. That's okay.

Now compute Xp = (1 + r2 - r1)/2. That's simple.

Now compute (x0 + r1). Let's compute that also in terms of r1,r2.

x0 + r1 = (1 + r1^2 - r2^2)/2 + r1 = (1 + r1^2 - r2^2 + 2r1)/2 = ( (r1^2 + 2r1 + 1) - r2^2 )/2 = ( (r1 + 1)^2 - r2^2 )/2.

Thus (x0 + r1) = \frac{ (r1+1)^2 - r2^2 }{2}.

|P|^2 = Xp^2 * (1 + (x0 + r1)^2 / y0^2) = Xp^2 * ( (y0^2 + (x0 + r1)^2) / y0^2 ).

|P|^2 = Xp^2 * (2 r1 (x0 + r1) ) / y0^2.

Thus

|P|^2 = \frac{2 r1 Xp^2 (x0 + r1)}{y0^2}.

Now also we have Xp = (1 + r2 - r1)/2. So Xp^2 = (1 + r2 - r1)^2 / 4.

Plug in:

|P|^2 = \frac{2 r1 * (1 + r2 - r1)^2 / 4 * (x0 + r1)}{y0^2} = \frac{ r1 (1 + r2 - r1)^2 (x0 + r1) }{2 y0^2}.

Now |v|^2 = |P|^2 + r1^2 + 2 r1 Xp.

Compute 2 r1 Xp = 2 r1 * (1 + r2 - r1)/2 = r1 (1 + r2 - r1). So

That equals radius. So L is tangent. Good.

Thus working in a coordinate where A is origin and MN is horizontal seems manageable. But we must be careful about the expressions for P and H. Let's set up that coordinate properly and then attempt to compute O (center of (BEF)) in terms of variables. I'll try to derive an analytic proof.

Let A = (0,0). Let MN be horizontal line y = h, where h ≠ 0. Without loss, we can assume h > 0 after reflecting across MN if necessary. Then M = (m_x, h), N = (n_x, h). Since A is on both circles, we have:

|M|^2 = m_x^2 + h^2 = r1^2   (1)
|N|^2 = n_x^2 + h^2 = r2^2   (2)

Now C is intersection of Ω (center M, radius r1) with MN, on the side opposite N. Since MN is horizontal, C lies on MN with x-coordinate m_x - r1 (to the left of M). So C = (m_x - r1, h). D is intersection of Γ with MN, on the side opposite M. So D = (n_x + r2, h). The order C - M - N - D along MN implies that m_x < n_x, and C is left of M, D is right of N. So that's okay.

Now P = circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since A = (0,0), we can compute P coordinates.

Let’s denote:

c = (c_x, h) where c_x = m_x - r1.
d = (d_x, h) where d_x = n_x + r2.

Then the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = (c_x + d_x)/2. So

P_x = (c_x + d_x)/2 = \frac{ (m_x - r1) + (n_x + r2) }{2} = \frac{ m_x + n_x + (r2 - r1) }{2}.

Now the perpendicular bisector of AC (or AD) gives the y-coordinate. Since A is origin, the condition |P|^2 = |P - C|^2 yields:

P_x^2 + P_y^2 = (P_x - c_x)^2 + (P_y - h)^2.

Expand:

P_x^2 + P_y^2 = P_x^2 - 2 P_x c_x + c_x^2 + P_y^2 - 2 P_y h + h^2.

Cancel P_x^2 + P_y^2:

0 = -2 P_x c_x + c_x^2 - 2 P_y h + h^2.

Thus

2 P_x c_x + 2 P_y h = c_x^2 + h^2.   (3)

Now we can compute c_x^2 + h^2. Since (m_x, h) satisfies (1): m_x^2 + h^2 = r1^2. Then

c_x^2 + h^2 = (m_x - r1)^2 + h^2 = m_x^2 - 2 m_x r1 + r1^2 + h^2 = (m_x^2 + h^2) + r1^2 - 2 m_x r1 = r1^2 + r1^2 - 2 m_x r1 = 2 r1^2 - 2 m_x r1 = 2 r1 (r1 - m_x). Good.

Now also note that P_x is expressed. So we have an equation linking P_y and P_x.

We can solve for P_y:

2 P_y h = c_x^2 + h^2 - 2 P_x c_x. So

P_y = \frac{ c_x^2 + h^2 - 2 P_x c_x }{ 2 h }.

We'll also need P·M and P·N. Since A is origin, P·M = P_x m_x + P_y h. Similarly P·N = P_x n_x + P_y h.

Now E = (2 P·M / |P|^2) P, F = (2 P·N / |P|^2) P. So E and F are multiples of P.

Now B is the reflection of A across MN. Since A is origin, reflecting across the line y = h yields B = (0, 2h). Indeed, the line y = h is horizontal. The foot from origin to that line is (0, h). So reflection gives B = (0, 2h). Good.

Now H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. We have P, M, N coordinates. Since MN is horizontal (y = h), the altitude from P is vertical, so H_x = P_x. Also altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So we can find H_y.

Let’s compute H_y. We have H = (P_x, y_h). Since H is on the vertical line through P, it's enough to use that MH ⟂ PN.

Compute vectors:

M - H = (m_x - P_x, h - y_h). PN = N - P = (n_x - P_x, h - P_y). Dot = 0.

So (m_x - P_x)*(n_x - P_x) + (h - y_h)*(h - P_y) = 0.   (4)

We can solve for y_h.

But perhaps we can also use formula for orthocenter in terms of coordinates: In triangle with vertices (x1,y1), (x2,y2), (x3,y3), the orthocenter is (x1+x2+x3 - 2*O_x, y1+y2+y3 - 2*O_y) where O is circumcenter. That's messy.

Alternatively, use the fact that the reflection of H across MN lies on the circumcircle of PMN. But we can directly compute y_h from (4). Since we know m_x, n_x, P_x, P_y, h. So we can derive an expression.

Let’s compute (4):

(m_x - P_x)*(n_x - P_x) + (h - y_h)*(h - P_y) = 0.

Thus (h - y_h)*(h - P_y) = - (m_x - P_x)*(n_x - P_x). So

h - y_h = - \frac{ (m_x - P_x)*(n_x - P_x) }{ h - P_y }.

Then y_h = h + \frac{ (m_x - P_x)*(n_x - P_x) }{ h - P_y }. (Note sign: minus times negative gives positive). Let's do carefully:

(m_x - P_x)*(n_x - P_x) + (h - y_h)*(h - P_y) = 0 => (h - y_h)*(h - P_y) = - (m_x - P_x)*(n_x - P_x). So

h - y_h = - \frac{ (m_x - P_x)*(n_x - P_x) }{ h - P_y }.

Thus y_h = h + \frac{ (m_x - P_x)*(n_x - P_x) }{ h - P_y }.

Now note that h - P_y may be zero? Possibly. But we need to check. In numeric example, P_y = -0.75, h=0, so h - P_y = 0.75, nonzero. So it's fine.

Now we also have the line L through H parallel to AP: direction P. So L: (x,y) = (P_x, y_h) + t (P_x, P_y). Its equation.

Now the circle (BEF): B = (0,2h), E = e P, F = f P, with e = 2 (P·M) / |P|^2, f = 2 (P·N) / |P|^2. So we have three points.

Now we need to prove that L is tangent to the circle passing through B, E, F. That is, distance from the center O of (BEF) to L equals radius. The circle (BEF) can be described as the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Since E and F are on the same line through origin, we can perhaps find a simple equation for that circle. Let's attempt to find the circle in terms of coordinates. Since A is origin, P is some vector, and E and F are scalar multiples of P. So points E and F lie on the line L0 (through origin) with direction P. The circle passing through B (on y-axis) and two points on L0. This circle is determined. Now also from (1): m_x^2 + h^2 = r1^2 => m_x^2 = r1^2 - h^2. Since r1 > h? Actually r1 > h because the distance from A to M is r1, and the foot is (0,h) with distance sqrt(m_x^2 + h^2) = r1. So indeed r1^2 = m_x^2 + h^2. Since h>0, r1 > h. Similarly, r2 > h.

Now we can also derive expressions for r1 and r2 in terms of m_x, n_x, h. But we might not need explicit m_x and n_x; we can treat them as variables.

Now P: P_x = (m_x + n_x + r2 - r1) / 2. But note that r1 and r2 are expressed in terms of m_x, n_x, h. So we can compute P_x.

We also have P_y = \frac{ c_x^2 + h^2 - 2 P_x c_x }{ 2 h }, where c_x = m_x - r1.

We can simplify c_x^2 + h^2 = 2 r1 (r1 - m_x). Also c_x = m_x - r1. So -2 P_x c_x = -2 P_x (m_x - r1). So

P_y = \frac{ 2 r1 (r1 - m_x) - 2 P_x (m_x - r1) }{ 2 h } = \frac{ 2 r1 (r1 - m_x) + 2 P_x (r1 - m_x) }{ 2 h } because (m_x - r1) = -(r1 - m_x). So

P_y = \frac{ 2 (r1 - m_x) (r1 + P_x) }{ 2 h } = \frac{ (r1 - m_x) (r1 + P_x) }{ h }.

Thus

P_y = \frac{ (r1 - m_x) (r1 + P_x) }{ h }.   (5)

Now r1 - m_x is something. Since m_x^2 + h^2 = r1^2, we have (r1 - m_x)(r1 + m_x) = r1^2 - m_x^2 = h^2. So r1 - m_x = h^2 / (r1 + m_x). That's not so simple. But we can keep as is.

Now also note that P_x is expressed. We might also find an expression for P_y in terms of m_x, n_x, h, r1, r2. Let's try to simplify P_y further by substituting P_x.

P_x = \frac{ m_x + n_x + (r2 - r1) }{ 2 }.

Then r1 + P_x = r1 + \frac{ m_x + n_x + r2 - r1 }{ 2 } = \frac{ 2 r1 + m_x + n_x + r2 - r1 }{ 2 } = \frac{ r1 + m_x + n_x + r2 }{ 2 }.

So r1 + P_x = \frac{ (r1 + r2) + (m_x + n_x) }{ 2 }.

Similarly, r1 - m_x is something.

Now P_y = \frac{ (r1 - m_x) (r1 + P_x) }{ h }. That's a rational expression. We'll also need |P|^2 = P_x^2 + P_y^2.

Perhaps we can find an equation for the circle (BEF) in terms of coordinates. Let’s set up coordinates with A = (0,0). We have variables: h, m_x, n_x. But we also have r1 = sqrt(m_x^2 + h^2), r2 = sqrt(n_x^2 + h^2). So we have three independent parameters (m_x, n_x, h) subject to m_x ≥ 0, n_x > m_x, and the intersecting condition automatically satisfied? Actually for circles to intersect, we need the distance between centers d = |n_x - m_x| (since y-coordinate same) to be less than r1 + r2 and greater than |r1 - r2|. That's a condition that is not automatically satisfied. But we can treat them as parameters that satisfy those inequalities. So we have three parameters.

Now we need to prove that L (through H parallel to P) is tangent to (BEF). That's a statement about these parameters. This seems like a lot of algebra. However maybe we can simplify by noticing that the expression for P_y simplifies nicely. Let's compute P_y more directly using the formula from (3). I'll compute P_y in terms of m_x, n_x, h, r1, r2. But perhaps we can find that P_y is something like - (something) * (m_x + n_x + something). Let's compute explicitly.

We have c_x = m_x - r1. Also P_x = (m_x + n_x + r2 - r1)/2. Then compute 2 P_x c_x = (m_x + n_x + r2 - r1)*(m_x - r1). That's messy. But we have expression (5): P_y = (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x) / h. That's okay.

Now also compute |P|^2. Maybe we can find a simpler expression for P·M.

P·M = P_x m_x + P_y h. Using (5), P_y h = (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x). So

P·M = P_x m_x + (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x) = P_x m_x + (r1 - m_x) r1 + (r1 - m_x) P_x = P_x m_x + r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1 - P_x m_x = (P_x m_x - P_x m_x) + r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1 = r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1.

Thus

P·M = r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1 = r1 (r1 - m_x + P_x).

Similarly, P·N = P_x n_x + P_y h = P_x n_x + (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x). That's not as neat. But maybe we can also express P·N using symmetry? Since N is on the same horizontal line, but not symmetric. However we can also compute P·N using the formula for P_y derived from (3) which involved c_x. That gave P_y = (c_x^2 + h^2 - 2 P_x c_x)/(2h). That's symmetrical. But maybe we can also derive an expression similar to (5) but with r2 and n_x? Since P is circumcenter of ACD, and D is on the right, we could also use the perpendicular bisector of AD. That would give an equation involving D. That might yield an expression for P_y in terms of r2 and n_x as well. Let's do that.

Consider the condition |P|^2 = |P - D|^2. That gives:

P_x^2 + P_y^2 = (P_x - d_x)^2 + (P_y - h)^2, where d_x = n_x + r2. Then

0 = -2 P_x d_x + d_x^2 - 2 P_y h + h^2. So

2 P_x d_x + 2 P_y h = d_x^2 + h^2.   (3')

Now d_x^2 + h^2 = (n_x + r2)^2 + h^2 = n_x^2 + 2 n_x r2 + r2^2 + h^2. But n_x^2 + h^2 = r2^2. So d_x^2 + h^2 = r2^2 + 2 n_x r2 + r2^2 = 2 r2^2 + 2 n_x r2 = 2 r2 (r2 + n_x). So that's 2 r2 (r2 + n_x). Meanwhile, 2 P_x d_x = 2 P_x (n_x + r2). So

2 P_x (n_x + r2) + 2 P_y h = 2 r2 (r2 + n_x). Cancel 2:

P_x (n_x + r2) + P_y h = r2 (r2 + n_x).   (6)

Now also from (3) we have P_x (m_x - r1) + P_y h = r1 (r1 - m_x). That's similar but with sign. Let's derive both.

From (3): 2 P_x c_x + 2 P_y h = c_x^2 + h^2 => P_x (m_x - r1) + P_y h = r1 (r1 - m_x). (since c_x^2 + h^2 = 2 r1 (r1 - m_x), and dividing by 2 gives P_x (m_x - r1) + P_y h = r1 (r1 - m_x).) But note that we have P_x (m_x - r1) = - P_x (r1 - m_x). So

- P_x (r1 - m_x) + P_y h = r1 (r1 - m_x). Then P_y h = (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x). That's (5). Good.

Now from (6): P_x (n_x + r2) + P_y h = r2 (r2 + n_x). So

P_y h = r2 (r2 + n_x) - P_x (n_x + r2). That's an expression.

Now we can also compute P_y h using (5). So we have two equations that must be consistent. That's fine.

Now we also have P·M = P_x m_x + P_y h. Using (5), P_y h = (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x). So

P·M = P_x m_x + (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x). Simplify: P_x m_x + (r1 - m_x) r1 + (r1 - m_x) P_x = P_x m_x + r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1 - P_x m_x = r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1. So that's (r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1). That's fine.

Similarly, P·N = P_x n_x + P_y h. Using (5), P_y h = (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x). So

P·N = P_x n_x + (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x) = P_x n_x + r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1 - P_x m_x.

Simplify: P_x (n_x - m_x) + r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1 = P_x (n_x - m_x) + r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1.

We can also use (6) to get an expression: P·N = P_x n_x + P_y h = P_x n_x + [r2 (r2 + n_x) - P_x (n_x + r2)] = r2 (r2 + n_x) - P_x r2. Because P_x n_x - P_x n_x cancels? Let's compute:

P·N = P_x n_x + P_y h. But from (6): P_y h = r2 (r2 + n_x) - P_x (n_x + r2). So

P·N = P_x n_x + r2 (r2 + n_x) - P_x (n_x + r2) = P_x n_x + r2 (r2 + n_x) - P_x n_x - P_x r2 = r2 (r2 + n_x) - P_x r2 = r2 (r2 + n_x - P_x).

Thus

P·N = r2 (r2 + n_x - P_x).   (7)

That's a nice expression. Similarly, we can get P·M = r1 (r1 - m_x + P_x)? Let's compute using (3). We have P·M = P_x m_x + P_y h. From (3): P_x (m_x - r1) + P_y h = r1 (r1 - m_x). So P_y h = r1 (r1 - m_x) - P_x (m_x - r1) = r1 (r1 - m_x) + P_x (r1 - m_x) = (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x). That's (5). Then

P·M = P_x m_x + (r1 - m_x)(r1 + P_x) = P_x m_x + (r1 - m_x) r1 + (r1 - m_x) P_x = P_x m_x + r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1 - P_x m_x = r1^2 - m_x r1 + P_x r1 = r1 (r1 - m_x + P_x). So

P·M = r1 (r1 - m_x + P_x).   (8)

Now also we have from (6): P·N = r2 (r2 + n_x - P_x). That's (7). So indeed P·M and P·N have symmetric forms.

P = circumcenter of ACD. A=0, C and D are points. So P is the circumcenter of triangle with vertices at 0, C, D. That's a triangle. That's the same as |X|^2 = |X - C|^2 => 2 X·C = |C|^2. Similarly, 2 X·D = |D|^2. So P satisfies:

2 P·C = |C|^2,   (I)
2 P·D = |D|^2.   (II)

Thus P is the unique point (if C and D are not collinear with origin) that satisfies these linear equations. Indeed, these are two linear equations in the coordinates of P. Since C and D are two vectors in the plane, and they are not parallel (they are collinear? Actually C and D lie on the same horizontal line y = h. So they are vectors with same y-coordinate h. They are not multiples unless they have same x-coordinate? They have different x-coordinates, so they are not collinear with the origin? Actually they are vectors from origin to points on line y = h. They are not multiples because they have same y-coordinate but different x-coordinates; however they are not scalar multiples unless one is a scalar multiple of the other. For two vectors (c_x, h) and (d_x, h) to be parallel, they must have the same direction, which would require that (c_x, h) = λ (d_x, h). That would imply h = λ h, so λ = 1 (if h ≠ 0). Then c_x = d_x. So if c_x ≠ d_x, they are not parallel. So indeed C and D are not collinear with the origin (they are not multiples). So the system (I),(II) has a unique solution P. That's nice.

Now we have:

Thus:

2 P·C = c_x^2 + h^2.
2 P·D = d_x^2 + h^2.

Now P is unknown. We can solve for P. Let’s denote P = (x,y). Then:

2 (x c_x + y h) = c_x^2 + h^2.   (1)
2 (x d_x + y h) = d_x^2 + h^2.   (2)

Subtract (1) from (2): 2 x (d_x - c_x) = (d_x^2 + h^2) - (c_x^2 + h^2) = d_x^2 - c_x^2. So

2 x (d_x - c_x) = (d_x - c_x)(d_x + c_x). Since d_x - c_x > 0. Divide by (d_x - c_x):

2 x = d_x + c_x. Thus

x = (d_x + c_x)/2 = \frac{ (q + r2) + (p - r1) }{2} = \frac{ p + q + (r2 - r1) }{2}. That's exactly the Xp we had earlier. So that's consistent.

Now plug back into (1) to solve for y:

2 ( x c_x + y h ) = c_x^2 + h^2. So

x c_x + y h = \frac{ c_x^2 + h^2 }{2}. Thus

y h = \frac{ c_x^2 + h^2 }{2} - x c_x. Then

y = \frac{ \frac{ c_x^2 + h^2 }{2} - x c_x }{ h }.

Now we have x expressed, and c_x expressed. So we can compute y. That's (9) earlier. But maybe we can find a nicer expression for y using the relationship (I) and (II). Since we have two linear equations, we can solve directly. Let's solve:

2 (α |u|^2 + β (u·v)) = |u|^2 => α |u|^2 + β (u·v) = \frac{ |u|^2 }{2}.  (A)
2 (α (u·v) + β |v|^2) = |v|^2 => α (u·v) + β |v|^2 = \frac{ |v|^2 }{2}.  (B)

v·b = v·b.

b·b = |b|^2.

Now u·(b - e u) = u·b - e |u|^2.
v·(b - e u) = v·b - e v·u = v·b (since v·u=0).
(b/2)·(b - e u) = (1/2)(b·b - e b·u) = (1/2)(|b|^2 - e (b·u)).

Thus:

(α - e/2)(u·b - e |u|^2) + β (v·b) - \frac{1}{2}(|b|^2 - e (b·u)) = 0.

Now α is known: α = (e+f)/2. So α - e/2 = (e+f)/2 - e/2 = f/2. So the coefficient simplifies nicely! That's good.

\frac{f}{2} (u·b - e |u|^2) + β (v·b) - \frac{1}{2}(|b|^2 - e (b·u)) = 0.

Multiply both sides by 2 to avoid fractions:

f (u·b - e |u|^2) + 2β (v·b) - (|b|^2 - e (b·u)) = 0.

Similarly, |X - A|^2 = |X - D|^2 gives:

2d X_x - 2a X_y = d^2 - a^2.   (Eq2)

Subtract Eq1 from Eq2: 2(d - c) X_x = (d^2 - a^2) - (c^2 - a^2) = d^2 - c^2 = (d - c)(d + c). Since d - c ≠ 0, divide by (d - c): 2 X_x = d + c → X_x = (c + d)/2, as before.

Now plug X_x into Eq1 to solve for X_y:

2c * ((c+d)/2) - 2a X_y = c^2 - a^2 → c(c+d) - 2a X_y = c^2 - a^2 → c^2 + c d - 2a X_y = c^2 - a^2 → cancel c^2: c d - 2a X_y = - a^2 → -2a X_y = - a^2 - c d → 2a X_y = a^2 + c d → X_y = (a^2 + c d) / (2a). So

Now the orthocenter H of triangle PMN: vertices P, M, N. Since M and N are on the x-axis, the altitude from P is vertical. So H_x = P_x. Also, we can compute H_y using the condition that MH ⟂ PN. Let's compute H_y. We have H = (P_x, y_H). Condition (M - H)·(P - N) = 0. Compute:

M - H = (m - P_x, 0 - y_H) = (m - P_x, -y_H).
P - N = (P_x - n, P_y - 0) = (P_x - n, P_y).

Dot = (m - P_x)*(P_x - n) + (-y_H)*P_y = 0 → (m - P_x)*(P_x - n) - y_H P_y = 0 → y_H P_y = (m - P_x)*(P_x - n). Thus

y_H = \frac{ (m - P_x)*(P_x - n) }{ P_y }.

That's a formula. Now note that P_y could be negative (as in numeric). That's okay.

- AP is the x-axis. So P = (p,0) with p > 0.
- M and N are centers of Ω and Γ, both passing through A. So |M| = r1, |N| = r2.
- The line MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB, and A is origin. So B is the reflection of A across MN. Since A=0, B = 2 H0, where H0 is the foot of perpendicular from origin to line MN. So H0 is the projection of origin onto MN. So B is twice that foot.
- C is the second intersection of Ω with MN, on the side opposite N. Since Ω passes through A and C, and M is center. So C is on MN, and also on Ω. So C is the point on MN such that |C - M| = r1. Similarly, D is on MN such that |D - N| = r2.
- The order C - M - N - D along MN. So M and N lie on segment CD.

Now, we have that AP is the x-axis. That's a choice. So P is on x-axis. But we also have that P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. So we need to relate P (which is (p,0)) to M,N,C,D. That will impose conditions. But maybe we can find expressions for M_x and N_x in terms of p and the geometry. Let's explore.

We have:

M = (M_x, M_y). N = (N_x, N_y). Since A=0 is on both circles, |M|^2 = M_x^2 + M_y^2 = r1^2, |N|^2 = N_x^2 + N_y^2 = r2^2.

Now, C is on MN. So we can param MN. Let’s denote the line MN. Its direction vector is u = (N - M). The foot H0 from origin to this line is some point. Then B = 2 H0.

Now, the chord AE of Ω lies on the x-axis. Since E is the second intersection, we have E = (2 M_x, 0). So that's a point on the x-axis. Similarly, F = (2 N_x, 0). So points E and F are on the x-axis. Thus triangle BEF has B off the x-axis, and E,F on the x-axis. The circumcenter O of triangle BEF can be found as the intersection of perpendicular bisector of EF (which is vertical line x = (2 M_x + 2 N_x)/2 = M_x + N_x) and perpendicular bisector of BE. So O has x-coordinate = M_x + N_x. That's nice.

Now, the line L is through H parallel to AP. AP is x-axis. So L is horizontal, i.e., y = y_H. So we need to prove that the horizontal line y = y_H is tangent to the circle passing through B, E, F. That means that the distance from the center O (which is (M_x + N_x, y_O)) to this horizontal line equals the radius R. So we need to compute y_O and R.

Now, we also need H, the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since we have coordinates for P, M, N. We can compute H_y. Let's compute H.

Triangle PMN: vertices P = (p,0), M = (M_x, M_y), N = (N_x, N_y). Since AP is x-axis, and P is on x-axis. The altitude from P is perpendicular to MN. So H lies on the line through P perpendicular to MN. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So H is the intersection.

But perhaps we can find an expression for H_y in terms of M_x, N_x, etc. There is a formula for orthocenter in coordinates: If triangle has vertices (x1,y1), (x2,y2), (x3,y3), then the orthocenter is (x1+x2+x3 - 2*O_x, y1+y2+y3 - 2*O_y) where O is circumcenter. That's messy. However, we can compute H by solving the linear equations derived from dot products: (M - H)·(P - N) = 0 and (N - H)·(P - M) = 0. Since we also know H lies on the line through P perpendicular to MN. But we can try to compute H_y directly.

Let’s denote H = (h_x, h_y). Since AP is horizontal, the line through H parallel to AP is horizontal. So we need to compute h_y. The orthocenter of triangle PMN is the point such that (M - H)·(P - N) = 0 and (N - H)·(P - M) = 0. Also, H is the intersection of altitudes. We can solve these equations.
